<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Caffeine</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A central nervous system stimulant</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the stimulant drug. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Caffeine_(disambiguation)" title="Caffeine (disambiguation)">Caffeine (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ddd"><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Caffeine_(data_page)" title="Caffeine (data page)">data page</a></i></th></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Caffeine</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS) <a href="/wiki/Stimulant" title="Stimulant">stimulant</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylxanthine" title="Methylxanthine">methylxanthine</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_classification" title="Chemical classification">class</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1356551_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1356551-10">[10]</a></sup> It is the world's most widely consumed <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive_drug" title="Psychoactive drug">psychoactive drug</a>. Unlike many other psychoactive substances, it is legal and unregulated in nearly all parts of the world. There are several known <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_of_action" title="Mechanism of action">mechanisms of action</a> to explain the effects of caffeine. The most prominent is that it reversibly blocks the action of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine" title="Adenosine">adenosine</a> on its receptor and consequently prevents the onset of drowsiness induced by adenosine. Caffeine also stimulates certain portions of the <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic nervous system</a>.
</p><p>Caffeine is a bitter, white crystalline <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">purine</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylxanthine" title="Methylxanthine">methylxanthine</a> <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloid</a>, and is chemically related to the <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> bases of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">deoxyribonucleic acid</a> (DNA) and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">ribonucleic acid</a> (RNA). It is found in the seeds, nuts, or leaves of a number of plants native to Africa, East Asia and South America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caballero_Finglas_Toldra_2015_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero_Finglas_Toldra_2015-11">[11]</a></sup> and helps to protect them against predator insects and to prevent germination of nearby seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myers_2007_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Myers_2007-12">[12]</a></sup> The most well-known source of caffeine is the <a href="/wiki/Coffee_bean" title="Coffee bean">coffee bean</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Misnomer" title="Misnomer">misnomer</a> for the seed of <i><a href="/wiki/Coffea" title="Coffea">Coffea</a></i> plants.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beverages" title="Beverages">Beverages</a> containing caffeine are ingested to relieve or prevent drowsiness and to improve performance. To make these drinks, caffeine is extracted by <a href="/wiki/Steeping" title="Steeping">steeping</a> the plant product in water, a process called <a href="/wiki/Infusion" title="Infusion">infusion</a>. Caffeine-containing drinks, such as <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cola" title="Cola">cola</a>, are very popular; as of 2014, 85% of American adults consumed some form of caffeine daily, consuming 164 mg on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine can have both positive and negative health effects. It can treat and prevent the premature infant breathing disorders <a href="/wiki/Bronchopulmonary_dysplasia" title="Bronchopulmonary dysplasia">bronchopulmonary dysplasia</a> of prematurity and <a href="/wiki/Apnea_of_prematurity" title="Apnea of prematurity">apnea of prematurity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Caffeine_citrate" title="Caffeine citrate">Caffeine citrate</a> is on the <a href="/wiki/WHO_Model_List_of_Essential_Medicines" title="WHO Model List of Essential Medicines">WHO Model List of Essential Medicines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOMedList_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOMedList-14">[14]</a></sup> It may confer a modest protective effect against some diseases,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qi_2014_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qi_2014-16">[16]</a></sup> Some people experience <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">sleep disruption</a> or anxiety if they consume caffeine, but others show little disturbance. Evidence of a risk during pregnancy is equivocal; some authorities recommend that pregnant women limit consumption to the equivalent of two cups of coffee per day or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MayoPreg2_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-MayoPreg2-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACOG_policy_462_caffeine_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACOG_policy_462_caffeine-18">[18]</a></sup> Caffeine can produce a mild form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_dependence" title="Drug dependence">drug dependence</a> – associated with <a href="/wiki/Drug_withdrawal" title="Drug withdrawal">withdrawal symptoms</a> such as sleepiness, headache, and irritability – when an individual stops using caffeine after repeated daily intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM-5_definitions_+_addiction-dependence_distinction_+_Caffeine_use_disorder_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-5_definitions_+_addiction-dependence_distinction_+_Caffeine_use_disorder-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15448977_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15448977-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">Tolerance</a> to the autonomic effects of increased blood pressure and heart rate, and increased urine output, develops with chronic use (i.e., these symptoms become less pronounced or do not occur following consistent use).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine is classified by the US <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> as "<a href="/wiki/Generally_recognized_as_safe" title="Generally recognized as safe">generally recognized as safe</a>" (GRAS). Toxic doses, over 10 grams per day for an adult, are much higher than the typical dose of under 500 milligrams per day. A cup of coffee contains 80–175 mg of caffeine, depending on what "bean" (seed) is used and how it is prepared (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drip_brew" title="Drip brew">drip</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coffee_percolator" title="Coffee percolator">percolation</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Espresso" title="Espresso">espresso</a>). Thus it requires roughly 50–100 ordinary cups of coffee to reach the toxic dose.  However, pure powdered caffeine, which is available as a <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplement</a>, can be lethal in tablespoon-sized amounts.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use">Use</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caffeine_citrate" title="Caffeine citrate">Caffeine citrate</a></div>
<p>Caffeine is used in:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bronchopulmonary_dysplasia" title="Bronchopulmonary dysplasia">Bronchopulmonary dysplasia</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premature_birth" title="Premature birth">premature</a> infants for both prevention<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21815280_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21815280-20">[20]</a></sup> and treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16210843_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16210843-21">[21]</a></sup> It may improve weight gain during therapy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16707748_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16707748-22">[22]</a></sup> and reduce the incidence of cerebral palsy as well as reduce language and cognitive delay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caffeine_for_AOP_NEJM_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-caffeine_for_AOP_NEJM-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caffeine_for_AOP_JAMA_2012_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-caffeine_for_AOP_JAMA_2012-24">[24]</a></sup> On the other hand, subtle long-term side effects are possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Funk_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Funk-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Apnea_of_prematurity" title="Apnea of prematurity">Apnea of prematurity</a> as a primary treatment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21127467_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21127467-26">[26]</a></sup> but not prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPHAR_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUPHAR-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Orthostatic_hypotension" title="Orthostatic hypotension">Orthostatic hypotension</a> treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPHAR_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUPHAR-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhancing_performance">Enhancing performance</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive">Cognitive</span></h4>
<p>Caffeine is a <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> stimulant that reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">fatigue</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drowsiness" title="Drowsiness">drowsiness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1356551_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1356551-10">[10]</a></sup> At normal doses, caffeine has variable effects on learning and memory, but it generally improves <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_time" title="Reaction time">reaction time</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wakefulness" title="Wakefulness">wakefulness</a>, concentration, and <a href="/wiki/Motor_coordination" title="Motor coordination">motor coordination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-effects_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-effects-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cog10_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cog10-31">[31]</a></sup> The amount of caffeine needed to produce these effects varies from person to person, depending on body size and degree of tolerance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-effects_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-effects-30">[30]</a></sup> The desired effects arise approximately one hour after consumption, and the desired effects of a moderate dose usually subside after about three or four hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poleszak_2015_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Poleszak_2015-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine can delay or prevent <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a> and improves task performance during sleep deprivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21531247_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21531247-32">[32]</a></sup> Shift workers who use caffeine make fewer mistakes due to drowsiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20464765_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20464765-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic review</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> from 2014 found that concurrent caffeine and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L-theanine" title="L-theanine"><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">L</span></span>-theanine</a> use has synergistic psychoactive effects that promote alertness, attention, and <a href="/wiki/Task_switching_(psychology)" title="Task switching (psychology)">task switching</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caffeine_and_theanine_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-caffeine_and_theanine-34">[34]</a></sup> these effects are most pronounced during the first hour post-dose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caffeine_and_theanine_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-caffeine_and_theanine-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span></h4>
<p>Caffeine is a proven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ergogenic_aid" title="Ergogenic aid">ergogenic aid</a> in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects-35">[35]</a></sup> Caffeine improves athletic performance in <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_exercise" title="Aerobic exercise">aerobic</a> (especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endurance_sports" title="Endurance sports">endurance sports</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_exercise" title="Anaerobic exercise">anaerobic</a> conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects-35">[35]</a></sup> Moderate doses of caffeine (around 5 mg/kg<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects-35">[35]</a></sup>) can improve sprint performance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21058748_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21058748-36">[36]</a></sup> cycling and running time trial performance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects-35">[35]</a></sup> endurance (i.e., it delays the onset of <a href="/wiki/Muscle_fatigue" title="Muscle fatigue">muscle fatigue</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_fatigue" title="Central fatigue">central fatigue</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21411838_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21411838-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ergogenics_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ergogenics-38">[38]</a></sup> and cycling power output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-Peripheral_and_central_ergogenic_effects-35">[35]</a></sup> Caffeine increases <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">basal metabolic rate</a> in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7369170_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7369170-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2912010_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid2912010-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7486839_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7486839-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine improves muscular strength and power,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and may enhance muscular endurance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Caffeine also enhances performance on anaerobic tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Caffeine consumption before constant load exercise is associated with reduced perceived exertion.  While this effect is not present during to exhaustion exercise, performance is significantly enhanced.  This is congruent with caffeine reducing perceived exertion, because exercise to exhaustion should end at the same point of fatigue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_populations">Specific populations</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Adults">Adults</span></h4>
<p>For the general population of healthy adults, Health Canada advises a daily intake of no more than 400 mg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itsyourhealth_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-itsyourhealth-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span></h4>
<p>In healthy children, caffeine intake produces effects that are "modest and typically innocuous".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> There is no evidence that coffee stunts a child's growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> For children age 12 and under, Health Canada recommends a maximum daily caffeine intake of no more than 2.5 milligrams per kilogram of body weight. Based on average body weights of children, this translates to the following age-based intake limits:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itsyourhealth_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-itsyourhealth-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Age range
</th>
<th>Maximum recommended daily caffeine intake
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>4–6
</td>
<td>45 mg (slightly more than in 12 oz of a typical caffeinated soft drink)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>7–9
</td>
<td>62.5 mg
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>10–12
</td>
<td>85 mg (about ½ cup of coffee)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Adolescents">Adolescents</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Health_Canada" title="Health Canada">Health Canada</a> has not developed advice for adolescents because of insufficient data. However, they suggest that daily caffeine intake for this age group be no more than 2.5 mg/kg body weight. This is because the maximum adult caffeine dose may not be appropriate for light-weight adolescents or for younger adolescents who are still growing. The daily dose of 2.5 mg/kg body weight would not cause adverse health effects in the majority of adolescent caffeine consumers. This is a conservative suggestion since older and heavier weight adolescents may be able to consume adult doses of caffeine without suffering adverse effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itsyourhealth_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-itsyourhealth-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pregnancy_and_breastfeeding">Pregnancy and breastfeeding</span></h4>
<p>The UK <a href="/wiki/Food_Standards_Agency" title="Food Standards Agency">Food Standards Agency</a> has recommended that pregnant women should limit their caffeine intake, out of prudence, to less than 200 mg of caffeine a day – the equivalent of two cups of instant coffee, or one and a half to two cups of fresh coffee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Congress_of_Obstetricians_and_Gynecologists" title="American Congress of Obstetricians and Gynecologists">American Congress of Obstetricians and Gynecologists</a> (ACOG) concluded in 2010 that caffeine consumption is safe up to 200 mg per day in pregnant women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACOG_policy_462_caffeine_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ACOG_policy_462_caffeine-18">[18]</a></sup> For women who breastfeed, are pregnant, or may become pregnant, Health Canada recommends a maximum daily caffeine intake of no more than 300 mg, or a little over two 8 oz (237 mL) cups of coffee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itsyourhealth_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-itsyourhealth-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are conflicting reports in the scientific literature about caffeine use during pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19238414_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19238414-50">[50]</a></sup> A 2011 review found that caffeine during pregnancy does not appear to increase the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital_malformations" title="Congenital malformations">congenital malformations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriage</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Growth_retardation" title="Growth retardation">growth retardation</a> even when consumed in moderate to high amounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21370398_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21370398-51">[51]</a></sup> Other reviews, however, concluded that there is some evidence that higher caffeine intake by pregnant women may be associated with a higher risk of giving birth to a <a href="/wiki/Low_birth_weight" title="Low birth weight">low birth weight</a> baby,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and may be associated with a higher risk of pregnancy loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> A systematic review, analyzing the results of observational studies, suggests that women who consume large amounts of caffeine (greater than 300 mg/day) prior to becoming pregnant may have a higher risk of experiencing pregnancy loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adverse_effects">Adverse effects<span id="Side_effects"></span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_2">Physical</span></h3>
<p>Caffeine can increase blood pressure and cause <a href="/wiki/Vasoconstriction" title="Vasoconstriction">vasoconstriction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniels1998_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daniels1998-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bracco_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bracco-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahmud_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahmud-57">[57]</a></sup> Coffee and caffeine can affect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_motility" title="Gastrointestinal motility">gastrointestinal motility</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gastric_acid" title="Gastric acid">gastric acid</a> secretion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boekema1999_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boekema1999-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen1975_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen1975-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwood2004_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwood2004-60">[60]</a></sup> Caffeine in low doses may cause weak bronchodilation for up to four hours in asthmatics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20091514_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20091514-61">[61]</a></sup> In postmenopausal women, high caffeine consumption can accelerate <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">bone loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mp_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-mp-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caffeine_intake_increases_the_rate_of_bone_loss_in_elderly_women_and_interacts_with_vitamin_D_receptor_genotypes_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caffeine_intake_increases_the_rate_of_bone_loss_in_elderly_women_and_interacts_with_vitamin_D_receptor_genotypes-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Doses of caffeine equivalent to the amount normally found in standard servings of tea, coffee and carbonated soft drinks appear to have no diuretic action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diuretic_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-diuretic-64">[64]</a></sup> However, acute ingestion of caffeine in large doses (at least 250–300 mg, equivalent to the amount found in 2–3 cups of coffee or 5–8 cups of tea) results in a short-term stimulation of urine output in individuals who have been deprived of caffeine for a period of days or weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diuretic_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-diuretic-64">[64]</a></sup> This increase is due to both a <a href="/wiki/Polyuria" title="Polyuria">diuresis</a> (increase in water excretion) and a <a href="/wiki/Natriuresis" title="Natriuresis">natriuresis</a> (increase in saline excretion); it is mediated via proximal tubular adenosine receptor blockade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The acute increase in urinary output may increase the risk of <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a>. However, chronic users of caffeine develop a <a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">tolerance</a> to this effect and experience no increase in urinary output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-really_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-really-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armstrong2007_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-armstrong2007-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological">Psychological</span></h3>
<p>Minor undesired symptoms from caffeine ingestion not sufficiently severe to warrant a psychiatric diagnosis are common and include mild anxiety, jitteriness, insomnia, increased sleep latency, and reduced coordination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-effects_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-effects-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21346331_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21346331-68">[68]</a></sup> Caffeine can have negative effects on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxiety_disorders" title="Anxiety disorders">anxiety disorders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winston_2005_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winston_2005-69">[69]</a></sup> According to a 2011 literature review, caffeine use is positively associated with anxiety and panic disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> At high doses, typically greater than 300 mg, caffeine can both cause and worsen anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12204388_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12204388-71">[71]</a></sup> For some people, discontinuing caffeine use can significantly reduce anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2727208_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid2727208-72">[72]</a></sup> In moderate doses, caffeine has been associated with reduced symptoms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression">depression</a> and lower <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psyc10_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Psyc10-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some textbooks state that caffeine is a mild euphoriant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> others state that it is not a euphoriant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and one states that it is and is not a euphoriant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Caffeine-induced_anxiety_disorder" title="Caffeine-induced anxiety disorder">Caffeine-induced anxiety disorder</a> is a subclass of the <a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a> diagnosis of substance/medication-induced anxiety disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Add2014_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Add2014-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reinforcement_disorders">Reinforcement disorders</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Addiction">Addiction</span></h4>
<p>Whether caffeine can result in an addictive disorder depends on how addiction is defined.  Compulsive caffeine consumption under any circumstances has not been observed, and caffeine is therefore not generally considered addictive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> However, some diagnostic models, such as the <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/International_Statistical_Classification_of_Diseases_and_Related_Health_Problems#ICDM-9" title="International Statistical Classification of Diseases and Related Health Problems">ICDM-9</a></span> and <a href="/wiki/ICD-10" title="ICD-10">ICD-10</a>, include a classification of caffeine addiction under a broader diagnostic model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Some state that certain users can become addicted and therefore unable to decrease use even though they know there are negative health effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine does not appear to be a reinforcing stimulus, and some degree of aversion may actually occur, with people preferring placebo over caffeine in a study on drug abuse liability published in an NIDA research monograph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Some state that research does not provide support for an underlying biochemical mechanism for caffeine addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human-caffeine_NAcc_neuroimaging_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Human-caffeine_NAcc_neuroimaging-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paper_cited_by_preceding_book_ref_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paper_cited_by_preceding_book_ref-88">[88]</a></sup> Other research states it can affect the reward system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Caffeine addiction" was added to the ICDM-9 and ICD-10. However, its addition was contested with claims that this diagnostic model of caffeine addiction is not supported by evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinical_addiction_&amp;_dependence_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clinical_addiction_&amp;_dependence-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICD-10_F15.2_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICD-10_F15.2-90">[90]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Psychiatric_Association" title="American Psychiatric Association">American Psychiatric Association</a>'s <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a></span> does not include the diagnosis of a <i>caffeine addiction</i> but proposes criteria for the disorder for more study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Add2014_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Add2014-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dependence_and_withdrawal">Dependence and withdrawal<span id="Withdrawal"></span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caffeine_dependence" title="Caffeine dependence">Caffeine dependence</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Drug_withdrawal" title="Drug withdrawal">Withdrawal</a> can cause mild to clinically significant distress or impairment in daily functioning.  The frequency at which this occurs is self reported at 11%, but in lab tests only half of the people who report withdrawal actually experience it, casting doubt on many claims of dependence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19428492_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19428492-92">[92]</a></sup> Mild <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a> and withdrawal symptoms may occur upon abstinence, with greater than 100 mg caffeine per day, although these symptoms last no longer than a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction-1">[1]</a></sup> some symptoms associated with <a href="/wiki/Psychological_dependence" title="Psychological dependence">psychological dependence</a> may also occur during <a href="/wiki/Drug_withdrawal" title="Drug withdrawal">withdrawal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15448977_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15448977-5">[5]</a></sup> Caffeine dependence can involve withdrawal symptoms such as fatigue, headache, irritability, depressed mood, reduced contentedness, inability to concentrate, sleepiness or drowsiness, <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">stomach pain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arthralgia" title="Arthralgia">joint pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_caffeine_dependence-addiction-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15448977_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15448977-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ICD-10 includes a diagnostic model for <a href="/wiki/Caffeine_dependence" title="Caffeine dependence">caffeine dependence</a>, but the DSM-5 does not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM-5_definitions_+_addiction-dependence_distinction_+_Caffeine_use_disorder_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-5_definitions_+_addiction-dependence_distinction_+_Caffeine_use_disorder-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICD-10_F15.2_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-ICD-10_F15.2-90">[90]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Psychiatric_Association" title="American Psychiatric Association">APA</a>, which published the DSM-5, acknowledged that there was sufficient evidence in order to create a diagnostic model of caffeine dependence for the DSM-5, but they noted that the <a href="/wiki/Clinical_significance" title="Clinical significance">clinical significance</a> of this disorder is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM-5_definitions_+_addiction-dependence_distinction_+_Caffeine_use_disorder_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-5_definitions_+_addiction-dependence_distinction_+_Caffeine_use_disorder-3">[3]</a></sup> The DSM-5 instead lists "caffeine use disorder" in the <a href="/wiki/DSM-5#Section_III:_emerging_measures_and_models" title="DSM-5">emerging models</a> section of the manual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM-5_definitions_+_addiction-dependence_distinction_+_Caffeine_use_disorder_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-5_definitions_+_addiction-dependence_distinction_+_Caffeine_use_disorder-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">Tolerance</a> to the effects of caffeine occurs for caffeine induced elevations in <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> and the subjective feelings of nervousness.  <a href="/wiki/Sensitization" title="Sensitization">Sensitization</a>, the process whereby effects become more prominent with use, occurs for positive effects such as feelings of alertness and well being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19428492_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19428492-92">[92]</a></sup> Tolerance varies for daily, regular caffeine users and high caffeine users. High doses of caffeine (750 to 1200 mg/day spread throughout the day) have been shown to produce complete tolerance to some, but not all of the effects of caffeine. Doses as low as 100 mg/day, such as a 6 oz cup of coffee or two to three 12 oz servings of caffeinated soft-drink, may continue to cause sleep disruption, among other intolerances. Non-regular caffeine users have the least caffeine tolerance for sleep disruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caffeinedependence_JohnHopkins_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caffeinedependence_JohnHopkins-93">[93]</a></sup> Some coffee drinkers develop tolerance to its undesired sleep-disrupting effects, but others apparently do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fredholm_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fredholm-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_of_other_diseases">Risk of other diseases</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Coffee#Health_and_pharmacology" title="Coffee">Coffee § Health and pharmacology</a></div>
<p>A protective effect of caffeine against <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a> and dementia is possible but the evidence is inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20182026_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20182026-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> It may protect people from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_cirrhosis" title="Liver cirrhosis">liver cirrhosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19825397_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19825397-98">[98]</a></sup> Caffeine may lessen the severity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_mountain_sickness" title="Acute mountain sickness">acute mountain sickness</a> if taken a few hours prior to attaining a high altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20367483_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20367483-99">[99]</a></sup> One meta analysis has found that caffeine consumption is associated with a reduced risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Two meta analyses have reported that caffeine consumption is associated with a linear reduction in risk for <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">parkinson's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qi_2014_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Qi_2014-16">[16]</a></sup> Caffeine consumption may be associated with reduced risk of <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> although conflicting results have been reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine increases <a href="/wiki/Intraocular_pressure" title="Intraocular pressure">intraocular pressure</a> in those with <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma" title="Glaucoma">glaucoma</a> but does not appear to affect normal individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20706731_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20706731-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overdose">Overdose</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Main_symptoms_of_Caffeine_overdose.svg"><img alt="Torso of a young man with overlaid text of main side-effects of caffeine overdose." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1216" data-file-width="1111" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Main_symptoms_of_Caffeine_overdose.svg/290px-Main_symptoms_of_Caffeine_overdose.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Main_symptoms_of_Caffeine_overdose.svg/435px-Main_symptoms_of_Caffeine_overdose.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Main_symptoms_of_Caffeine_overdose.svg/580px-Main_symptoms_of_Caffeine_overdose.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Main_symptoms_of_Caffeine_overdose.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Primary symptoms of caffeine intoxication<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medline_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Medline-105">[105]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Consumption of 1–1.5 grams (0.035–0.053 oz) per day is associated with a condition known as <i><dfn><a href="/wiki/Caffeinism" title="Caffeinism">caffeinism</a></dfn>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Caffeinism usually combines caffeine <a href="/wiki/Substance_dependence" title="Substance dependence">dependency</a> with a wide range of unpleasant symptoms including nervousness, irritability, restlessness, insomnia, headaches, and palpitations after caffeine use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine overdose can result in a state of central nervous system over-stimulation called <i><dfn>caffeine intoxication</dfn></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DSM-IV" title="DSM-IV">DSM-IV</a> 305.90).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM-IV_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-IV-108">[108]</a></sup> This syndrome typically occurs only after ingestion of large amounts of caffeine, well over the amounts found in typical caffeinated beverages and caffeine tablets (e.g., more than 400–500 mg at a time). The symptoms of caffeine intoxication are comparable to the symptoms of overdoses of other <a href="/wiki/Stimulant" title="Stimulant">stimulants</a>: they may include restlessness, fidgeting, anxiety, excitement, insomnia, flushing of the face, increased urination, gastrointestinal disturbance, muscle twitching, a rambling flow of thought and speech, irritability, irregular or rapid heart beat, and <a href="/wiki/Psychomotor_agitation" title="Psychomotor agitation">psychomotor agitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medline_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Medline-105">[105]</a></sup> In cases of much larger overdoses, <a href="/wiki/Mania" title="Mania">mania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>, lapses in judgment, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disorientation" title="Disorientation">disorientation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disinhibition" title="Disinhibition">disinhibition</a>, delusions, hallucinations, or psychosis may occur, and <a href="/wiki/Rhabdomyolysis" title="Rhabdomyolysis">rhabdomyolysis</a> (breakdown of skeletal muscle tissue) can be provoked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verkhratsky_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verkhratsky-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Massive overdose can result in death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmgren_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmgren-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Median_lethal_dose" title="Median lethal dose">LD<sub>50</sub></a> of caffeine in humans is dependent on individual sensitivity, but is estimated to be 150–200 milligrams per kilogram of body mass (75–100 cups of coffee for a 70 kilogram adult).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ld50_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-ld50-113">[113]</a></sup> A number of fatalities have been caused by overdoses of readily available powdered caffeine supplements, for which the estimated lethal amount is less than a tablespoon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The lethal dose is lower in individuals whose ability to metabolize caffeine is impaired due to genetics or chronic liver disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liver-damage_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-liver-damage-115">[115]</a></sup> A death was reported in a man with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_cirrhosis" title="Liver cirrhosis">liver cirrhosis</a> who overdosed on caffeinated mints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent-2013-10-11_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-independent-2013-10-11-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph-2013-10-13_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-2013-10-13-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mirror-2013-10-12_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-mirror-2013-10-12-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Treatment of mild caffeine intoxication is directed toward symptom relief; severe intoxication may require <a href="/wiki/Peritoneal_dialysis" title="Peritoneal dialysis">peritoneal dialysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemodialysis" title="Hemodialysis">hemodialysis</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Hemofiltration" title="Hemofiltration">hemofiltration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medline_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-Medline-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions">Interactions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol">Alcohol</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Caffeinated_alcoholic_drink" title="Caffeinated alcoholic drink">Caffeinated alcoholic drink</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/DSST_(standardized_test)" title="DSST (standardized test)">DSST</a>, alcohol provides a reduction in performance and caffeine has a significant improvement in performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackay2002_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mackay2002-119">[119]</a></sup> When alcohol and caffeine are consumed jointly, the effects produced by caffeine are affected, but the alcohol effects remain the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liguori2001_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liguori2001-120">[120]</a></sup> For example, when additional caffeine is added, the drug effect produced by alcohol is not reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liguori2001_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Liguori2001-120">[120]</a></sup> However, the jitteriness and alertness given by caffeine is decreased when additional alcohol is consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liguori2001_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-Liguori2001-120">[120]</a></sup> Alcohol consumption alone reduces both inhibitory and activational aspects of behavioral control. Caffeine antagonizes the activational aspect of behavioral control, but has no effect on the inhibitory behavioral control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marczinski2003_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marczinski2003-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tobacco">Tobacco</span></h3>
<p>Smoking tobacco increases caffeine clearance by 56%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_control">Birth control</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_control_pills" title="Birth control pills">Birth control pills</a> can extend the half-life of caffeine, requiring greater attention to caffeine consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benowitz1990_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benowitz1990-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications">Medications</span></h3>
<p>Caffeine sometimes increases the effectiveness of some medications, such as those for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headaches" title="Headaches">headaches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21302868_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21302868-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacology">Pharmacology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacodynamics">Pharmacodynamics</span></h3>
<table class="infobox">

</th></tr>

<td><div style="position: relative;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SynapseSchematic_lines.svg"><img alt="An illustrated chemical synapse" data-file-height="1064" data-file-width="2000" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SynapseSchematic_lines.svg/400px-SynapseSchematic_lines.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SynapseSchematic_lines.svg/600px-SynapseSchematic_lines.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SynapseSchematic_lines.svg/800px-SynapseSchematic_lines.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
