</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_and_adenosine.svg"><img alt="Two skeletal formulas: left – caffeine, right – adenosine." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="512" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Caffeine_and_adenosine.svg/250px-Caffeine_and_adenosine.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Caffeine_and_adenosine.svg/375px-Caffeine_and_adenosine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Caffeine_and_adenosine.svg/500px-Caffeine_and_adenosine.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_and_adenosine.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caffeine's primary mechanism of action is as an <a href="/wiki/Receptor_antagonist" title="Receptor antagonist">antagonist</a> of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine" title="Adenosine">adenosine</a> receptors in the brain</div></div></div>
<p>In the absence of caffeine and when a person is awake and alert, little <a href="/wiki/Adenosine" title="Adenosine">adenosine</a> is present in (CNS) neurons. With a continued wakeful state, over time it accumulates in the neuronal <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapse</a>, in turn binding to and activating <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor" title="Adenosine receptor">adenosine receptors</a> found on certain CNS neurons; when activated, these receptors produce a cellular response that ultimately increases <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drowsiness" title="Drowsiness">drowsiness</a>. When caffeine is consumed, it <a href="/wiki/Receptor_antagonist" title="Receptor antagonist">antagonizes</a> adenosine receptors; in other words, caffeine prevents adenosine from activating the receptor by blocking the location on the receptor where adenosine binds to it. As a result, caffeine temporarily prevents or relieves drowsiness, and thus maintains or restores alertness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugbank-Caffeine_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-Drugbank-Caffeine-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Receptor_and_ion_channel_targets">Receptor and ion channel targets</span></h4>
<p>Caffeine is an <a href="/wiki/Receptor_antagonist" title="Receptor antagonist">antagonist</a> at all four <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor" title="Adenosine receptor">adenosine receptor</a> subtypes (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A1" title="Adenosine receptor A1">A<sub>1</sub></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A2a" title="Adenosine receptor A2a">A<sub>2A</sub></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A2b" title="Adenosine receptor A2b">A<sub>2B</sub></a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A3" title="Adenosine receptor A3">A<sub>3</sub></a>), although with varying <a href="/wiki/Potency_(pharmacology)" title="Potency (pharmacology)">potencies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugbank-Caffeine_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-Drugbank-Caffeine-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22886028_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22886028-125">[125]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affinity_(pharmacology)" title="Affinity (pharmacology)">affinity</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dissociation_constant" title="Dissociation constant">K<sub>D</sub></a>) values of caffeine for the human adenosine receptors are 12 μM at <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_A1_receptor" title="Adenosine A1 receptor">A<sub>1</sub></a>, 2.4 μM at <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_A2A_receptor" title="Adenosine A2A receptor">A<sub>2A</sub></a>, 13 μM at <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_A2B_receptor" title="Adenosine A2B receptor">A<sub>2B</sub></a>, and 80 μM at <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_A3_receptor" title="Adenosine A3 receptor">A<sub>3</sub></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22886028_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22886028-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Knockout_mouse" title="Knockout mouse">Knockout mouse</a> studies have specifically implicated antagonism of the A<sub>2A</sub> receptor as responsible for the wakefulness-promoting effects of caffeine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22886028_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22886028-125">[125]</a></sup> Antagonism of adenosine receptors by caffeine stimulates the <a href="/wiki/Medulla_oblongata" title="Medulla oblongata">medullary</a> vagal, vasomotor, and <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_center" title="Respiratory center">respiratory centers</a>, which increases respiratory rate, reduces heart rate, and constricts blood vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugbank-Caffeine_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-Drugbank-Caffeine-6">[6]</a></sup> Adenosine receptor antagonism also promotes neurotransmitter release (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoamines" title="Monoamines">monoamines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">acetylcholine</a>), which endows caffeine with its stimulant effects;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugbank-Caffeine_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-Drugbank-Caffeine-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adenosine" title="Adenosine">adenosine</a> acts as an inhibitory neurotransmitter that suppresses activity in the central nervous system. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_palpitation" title="Heart palpitation">Heart palpitations</a> are caused by blockade of the A<sub>1</sub> receptor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugbank-Caffeine_6-12"><a href="#cite_note-Drugbank-Caffeine-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because caffeine is both water- and lipid-soluble, it readily crosses the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a> that separates the bloodstream from the interior of the brain. Once in the brain, the principal mode of action is as a nonselective <a href="/wiki/Receptor_antagonist" title="Receptor antagonist">antagonist</a> of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor" title="Adenosine receptor">adenosine receptors</a> (in other words, an agent that reduces the effects of adenosine). The caffeine molecule is structurally similar to adenosine, and is capable of binding to adenosine receptors on the surface of cells without activating them, thereby acting as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Competitive_antagonist" title="Competitive antagonist">competitive antagonist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15095008_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15095008-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to its activity at adenosine receptors, caffeine is an <a href="/wiki/Inositol_trisphosphate_receptor" title="Inositol trisphosphate receptor">inositol trisphosphate receptor 1</a> antagonist and a voltage-independent activator of the <a href="/wiki/Ryanodine_receptor" title="Ryanodine receptor">ryanodine receptors</a> (<a href="/wiki/RYR1" title="RYR1">RYR1</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RYR2" title="RYR2">RYR2</a>, and <a href="/wiki/RYR3" title="RYR3">RYR3</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPHAR_caffeine_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUPHAR_caffeine-128">[128]</a></sup> It is also a competitive antagonist of the <a href="/wiki/Glycine_receptor" title="Glycine receptor">ionotropic glycine receptor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19564396_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19564396-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_striatal_dopamine">Effects on striatal dopamine</span></h5>
<p>While caffeine does not directly bind to any <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor" title="Dopamine receptor">dopamine receptors</a>, it influences the binding activity of <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> at its receptors in the <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">striatum</a> by binding to adenosine receptors that have formed <a href="/wiki/GPCR_oligomer" title="GPCR oligomer">GPCR heteromers</a> with dopamine receptors, specifically the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A1" title="Adenosine receptor A1">A<sub>1</sub></a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DRD1" title="DRD1">D<sub>1</sub></a> receptor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterodimer" title="Heterodimer">heterodimer</a> (this is a receptor complex with 1 adenosine A<sub>1</sub> receptor and 1 dopamine D<sub>1</sub> receptor) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A2a" title="Adenosine receptor A2a">A<sub>2A</sub></a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DRD2" title="DRD2">D<sub>2</sub></a> receptor <a href="/wiki/Heterotetramer" title="Heterotetramer">heterotetramer</a> (this is a receptor complex with 2 adenosine A<sub>2A</sub> receptors and 2 dopamine D<sub>2</sub> receptors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A1-DRD1_and_A2a-DRD2_review_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-A1-DRD1_and_A2a-DRD2_review-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caffeine_heterotetramer_review_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caffeine_heterotetramer_review-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caffeine_psychostimulant_effects_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caffeine_psychostimulant_effects-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caffeine_SUD_interactions_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caffeine_SUD_interactions-133">[133]</a></sup> The A<sub>2A</sub>–D<sub>2</sub> receptor heterotetramer has been identified as a primary pharmacological target of caffeine, primarily because it mediates some of its psychostimulant effects and its pharmacodynamic interactions with dopaminergic psychostimulants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caffeine_heterotetramer_review_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Caffeine_heterotetramer_review-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caffeine_psychostimulant_effects_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Caffeine_psychostimulant_effects-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caffeine_SUD_interactions_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Caffeine_SUD_interactions-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine also causes the release of dopamine in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsal_striatum" title="Dorsal striatum">dorsal striatum</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens_core" title="Nucleus accumbens core">nucleus accumbens core</a> (a substructure within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral_striatum" title="Ventral striatum">ventral striatum</a>), but not the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens_shell" title="Nucleus accumbens shell">nucleus accumbens shell</a>, by antagonizing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A1" title="Adenosine receptor A1">A<sub>1</sub></a> receptors in the <a href="/wiki/Axon_terminal" title="Axon terminal">axon terminal</a> of dopamine neurons and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A1" title="Adenosine receptor A1">A<sub>1</sub></a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosine_receptor_A2a" title="Adenosine receptor A2a">A<sub>2A</sub></a> heterodimers (a receptor complex composed of 1 adenosine A<sub>1</sub> receptor and 1 adenosine A<sub>2A</sub> receptor) in the axon terminal of glutamate neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A1-DRD1_and_A2a-DRD2_review_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-A1-DRD1_and_A2a-DRD2_review-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferre_2008_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferre_2008-134">[134]</a></sup> During chronic caffeine use, caffeine-induced dopamine release within the nucleus accumbens core is markedly reduced due to <a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">drug tolerance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A1-DRD1_and_A2a-DRD2_review_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-A1-DRD1_and_A2a-DRD2_review-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferre_2008_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ferre_2008-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Enzyme_targets">Enzyme targets</span></h4>
<p>Caffeine, like other <a href="/wiki/Xanthine" title="Xanthine">xanthines</a>, also acts as a <a href="/wiki/Phosphodiesterase_inhibitor" title="Phosphodiesterase inhibitor">phosphodiesterase inhibitor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20164566_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20164566-135">[135]</a></sup> As a competitive nonselective <a href="/wiki/Phosphodiesterase_inhibitor" title="Phosphodiesterase inhibitor">phosphodiesterase inhibitor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDEs-Essayan_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDEs-Essayan-136">[136]</a></sup> caffeine raises intracellular <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_adenosine_monophosphate" title="Cyclic adenosine monophosphate">cAMP</a>, activates <a href="/wiki/Protein_kinase_A" title="Protein kinase A">protein kinase A</a>, <a href="/wiki/TNF_inhibitor" title="TNF inhibitor">inhibits TNF-alpha</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PTX-Deree_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-PTX-Deree-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9927365_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9927365-138">[138]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Leukotriene" title="Leukotriene">leukotriene</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LT-Peters-Golden_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-LT-Peters-Golden-139">[139]</a></sup> synthesis, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-inflammatory" title="Anti-inflammatory">reduces inflammation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innate_immunity" title="Innate immunity">innate immunity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LT-Peters-Golden_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-LT-Peters-Golden-139">[139]</a></sup> Caffeine also affects the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cholinergic_system" title="Cholinergic system">cholinergic system</a> where it inhibits the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholinesterase" title="Acetylcholinesterase">acetylcholinesterase</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Off-target_effects">Off-target effects</span></h4>
<p>Caffeine antagonizes <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_A2A_receptor" title="Adenosine A2A receptor">adenosine A2A receptors</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventrolateral_preoptic_area" title="Ventrolateral preoptic area">ventrolateral preoptic area</a> (VLPO), thereby reducing inhibitory <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GABA" title="GABA">GABA</a> <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmission" title="Neurotransmission">neurotransmission</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Tuberomammillary_nucleus" title="Tuberomammillary nucleus">tuberomammillary nucleus</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Histamine" title="Histamine">histaminergic</a> projection nucleus that activation-dependently promotes arousal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferre_2008_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ferre_2008-134">[134]</a></sup> Disinhibition of the tuberomammillary nucleus is the chief mechanism by which caffeine produces wakefulness-promoting effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferre_2008_134-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ferre_2008-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacokinetics">Pharmacokinetics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_metabolites.svg"><img alt="A diagram featuring 4 skeletal chemical formulas. Top (caffeine) relates to similar compounds paraxanthine, theobromine and theophylline." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3627" data-file-width="4296" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Caffeine_metabolites.svg/290px-Caffeine_metabolites.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Caffeine_metabolites.svg/435px-Caffeine_metabolites.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Caffeine_metabolites.svg/580px-Caffeine_metabolites.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_metabolites.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caffeine is metabolized in the liver via a single <a href="/wiki/Demethylation" title="Demethylation">demethylation</a>, resulting in three primary metabolites, <a href="/wiki/Paraxanthine" title="Paraxanthine">paraxanthine</a> (84%), <a href="/wiki/Theobromine" title="Theobromine">theobromine</a> (12%), and <a href="/wiki/Theophylline" title="Theophylline">theophylline</a> (4%), depending on which methyl group is removed.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_metabolism.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3412" data-file-width="3006" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Caffeine_metabolism.png/290px-Caffeine_metabolism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Caffeine_metabolism.png/435px-Caffeine_metabolism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Caffeine_metabolism.png/580px-Caffeine_metabolism.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_metabolism.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Urinary metabolites of caffeine in humans at 48 hours post-dose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20859793_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20859793-140">[140]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Caffeine from coffee or other beverages is absorbed by the small intestine within 45 minutes of ingestion and distributed throughout all bodily tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9329065_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9329065-141">[141]</a></sup> Peak blood concentration is reached within 1–2 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> It is eliminated by <a href="/wiki/Rate_equation#First-order_reactions" title="Rate equation">first-order kinetics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7333346_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7333346-143">[143]</a></sup> Caffeine can also be absorbed rectally, evidenced by suppositories of <a href="/wiki/Ergotamine" title="Ergotamine">ergotamine</a> <a href="/wiki/Tartrate" title="Tartrate">tartrate</a> and caffeine (for the relief of <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">migraine</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid13165929_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid13165929-144">[144]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorobutanol" title="Chlorobutanol">chlorobutanol</a> and caffeine (for the treatment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperemesis" title="Hyperemesis">hyperemesis</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17553397_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17553397-145">[145]</a></sup> However, rectal absorption is less efficient than oral:  the maximum concentration (<a href="/wiki/Cmax_(pharmacology)" title="Cmax (pharmacology)">C<sub>max</sub></a>) and total amount absorbed (<a href="/wiki/Area_under_the_curve_(pharmacokinetics)" title="Area under the curve (pharmacokinetics)">AUC</a>) are both about 30% (i.e., 1/3.5) of the oral amounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine's <a href="/wiki/Biological_half-life" title="Biological half-life">biological half-life</a> – the time required for the body to eliminate one-half of a dose – varies widely among individuals according to factors such as pregnancy, other drugs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_enzymes" title="Liver enzymes">liver enzyme</a> function level (needed for caffeine metabolism) and age. In healthy adults, caffeine's half-life is between 3–7 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugbank-Caffeine_6-13"><a href="#cite_note-Drugbank-Caffeine-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">Smoking</a> decreases the half-life by 30–50%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fredholm_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fredholm-94">[94]</a></sup> while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_contraceptives" title="Oral contraceptives">oral contraceptives</a> can double it<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fredholm_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fredholm-94">[94]</a></sup> and pregnancy can raise it to as much as 15 hours during the third trimester.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fredholm_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fredholm-94">[94]</a></sup> In newborns the half-life can be 80 hours or more, dropping very rapidly with age, possibly to less than the adult value by age 6 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fredholm_94-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fredholm-94">[94]</a></sup> The antidepressant <a href="/wiki/Fluvoxamine" title="Fluvoxamine">fluvoxamine</a> (Luvox) reduces the clearance of caffeine by more than 90%, and increases its elimination half-life more than tenfold; from 4.9 hours to 56 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-half-life_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-half-life-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine is <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolized</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cytochrome_P450_oxidase" title="Cytochrome P450 oxidase">cytochrome P450 oxidase</a> enzyme system, in particular, by the <a href="/wiki/CYP1A2" title="CYP1A2">CYP1A2</a> isozyme, into three dimethyl<a href="/wiki/Xanthine" title="Xanthine">xanthines</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> each of which has its own effects on the body:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Paraxanthine" title="Paraxanthine">Paraxanthine</a> (84%): Increases <a href="/wiki/Lipolysis" title="Lipolysis">lipolysis</a>, leading to elevated <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> and free <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acid</a> levels in <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">blood plasma</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Theobromine" title="Theobromine">Theobromine</a> (12%): Dilates <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> and increases <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> volume. Theobromine is also the principal <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloid</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa bean</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">chocolate</a>).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Theophylline" title="Theophylline">Theophylline</a> (4%): Relaxes <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscles</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">bronchi</a>, and is used to treat <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therapeutic_dose" title="Therapeutic dose">therapeutic dose</a> of theophylline, however, is many times greater than the levels attained from caffeine metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20091514_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20091514-61">[61]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/1,3,7-Trimethyluric_acid" title="1,3,7-Trimethyluric acid">1,3,7-Trimethyluric acid</a> is a minor caffeine metabolite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugbank-Caffeine_6-14"><a href="#cite_note-Drugbank-Caffeine-6">[6]</a></sup> Each of these metabolites is further metabolized and then excreted in the urine. Caffeine can accumulate in individuals with severe <a href="/wiki/Liver_disease" title="Liver disease">liver disease</a>, increasing its half-life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18762933_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18762933-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2011 review found that increased caffeine intake was associated with a variation in two genes that increase the rate of caffeine catabolism. Subjects who had this <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutation</a> on both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromosomes" title="Chromosomes">chromosomes</a> consumed 40 mg more caffeine per day than others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibson2011_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibson2011-150">[150]</a></sup> This is presumably due to the need for a higher intake to achieve a comparable desired effect, not that the gene led to a disposition for greater incentive of habituation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h2>
<p>Pure <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> caffeine is a bitter-tasting, white, odorless powder with a melting point of 235–238 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pubchem_properties_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pubchem_properties-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChemSpider_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChemSpider-9">[9]</a></sup> Caffeine is moderately soluble in water at room temperature (2 g/100 mL), but very soluble in boiling water (66 g/100 mL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solu_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-solu-151">[151]</a></sup> It is also moderately soluble in ethanol (1.5 g/100 mL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solu_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-solu-151">[151]</a></sup> It is weakly basic (pK<sub>a</sub> of <a href="/wiki/Conjugate_acid" title="Conjugate acid">conjugate acid</a> = ~0.6) requiring strong acid to protonate it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pKa_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-pKa-152">[152]</a></sup> Caffeine does not contain any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stereogenic" title="Stereogenic">stereogenic</a> centers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-7614-2242-0_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-7614-2242-0-153">[153]</a></sup> and hence is classified as an <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">achiral</a> molecule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn1-58409-016-2_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn1-58409-016-2-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Xanthine" title="Xanthine">xanthine</a> core of caffeine contains two fused rings, a <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidinedione" title="Pyrimidinedione">pyrimidinedione</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imidazole" title="Imidazole">imidazole</a>.  The pyrimidinedione in turn contains two <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amide</a> functional groups that exist predominantly in a <a href="/wiki/Zwitterion" title="Zwitterion">zwitterionic</a> <a href="/wiki/Resonance_(chemistry)" title="Resonance (chemistry)">resonance</a> the location from which the nitrogen atoms are double bonded to their adjacent amide carbons atoms. Hence all six of the atoms within the pyrimidinedione ring system are sp<sup>2</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" title="Orbital hybridization">hybridized</a> and planar. Therefore, the fused 5,6 ring core of caffeine contains a total of ten <a href="/wiki/Pi_bond" title="Pi bond">pi electrons</a> and hence according to <a href="/wiki/H%C3%BCckel%27s_rule" title="Hückel's rule">Hückel's rule</a> is <a href="/wiki/Aromaticity" title="Aromaticity">aromatic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlquantum.esu.edu_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlquantum.esu.edu-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis">Synthesis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_biosynthesis.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1361" data-file-width="2537" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Caffeine_biosynthesis.tif/lossy-page1-290px-Caffeine_biosynthesis.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Caffeine_biosynthesis.tif/lossy-page1-435px-Caffeine_biosynthesis.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Caffeine_biosynthesis.tif/lossy-page1-580px-Caffeine_biosynthesis.tif.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_biosynthesis.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthetic route</a> of caffeine, as performed by <a href="/wiki/Camellia" title="Camellia">Camellia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coffea" title="Coffea">Coffea</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlcaffeine_biosynthesis_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlcaffeine_biosynthesis-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_synthesis.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1102" data-file-width="2646" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Caffeine_synthesis.tif/lossy-page1-290px-Caffeine_synthesis.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Caffeine_synthesis.tif/lossy-page1-435px-Caffeine_synthesis.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Caffeine_synthesis.tif/lossy-page1-580px-Caffeine_synthesis.tif.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caffeine_synthesis.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">laboratory synthesis</a> of caffeine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn1-58829-173-1_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn1-58829-173-1-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US2785162_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-US2785162-159">[159]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesis</a> of caffeine is an example of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a> among different species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine may be synthesized in the lab starting with <a href="/wiki/Dimethylurea" title="Dimethylurea">dimethylurea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malonic_acid" title="Malonic acid">malonic acid</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="refs are for multiple routes and image is not this route (March 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn1-58829-173-1_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-isbn1-58829-173-1-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US2785162_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-US2785162-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zajac_2003_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zajac_2003-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial supplies of caffeine are not usually manufactured synthetically because the chemical is readily available as a byproduct of decaffeination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decaffeination">Decaffeination</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Decaffeination" title="Decaffeination">Decaffeination</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CaffeineCrystals_Fibrous_10xDarkField.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4476" data-file-width="7362" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/CaffeineCrystals_Fibrous_10xDarkField.jpg/290px-CaffeineCrystals_Fibrous_10xDarkField.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/CaffeineCrystals_Fibrous_10xDarkField.jpg/435px-CaffeineCrystals_Fibrous_10xDarkField.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/CaffeineCrystals_Fibrous_10xDarkField.jpg/580px-CaffeineCrystals_Fibrous_10xDarkField.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CaffeineCrystals_Fibrous_10xDarkField.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fibrous <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a> of purified caffeine. <a href="/wiki/Dark-field_microscopy" title="Dark-field microscopy">Dark-field microscopy</a> image, about 7 mm × 11 mm</div></div></div>
<p>Extraction of caffeine from coffee, to produce caffeine and decaffeinated coffee, can be performed using a number of solvents. <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">Benzene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trichloroethylene" title="Trichloroethylene">trichloroethylene</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dichloromethane" title="Dichloromethane">dichloromethane</a> have all been used over the years but for reasons of safety, environmental impact, cost, and flavor, they have been superseded by the following main methods:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Water extraction:</b> Coffee beans are soaked in water. The water, which contains many other compounds in addition to caffeine and contributes to the flavor of coffee, is then passed through <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated charcoal</a>, which removes the caffeine. The water can then be put back with the beans and evaporated dry, leaving decaffeinated coffee with its original flavor. Coffee manufacturers recover the caffeine and resell it for use in soft drinks and over-the-counter caffeine tablets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Decaffeination_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Decaffeination-165">[165]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Supercritical carbon dioxide extraction:</b> <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">Supercritical carbon dioxide</a> is an excellent nonpolar <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> for caffeine, and is safer than the organic solvents that are otherwise used. The extraction process is simple: CO<sub>2</sub> is forced through the green coffee beans at temperatures above 31.1 °C and pressures above 73 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atm</a>. Under these conditions, CO<sub>2</sub> is in a "<a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a>" <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">state</a>: It has gaslike properties that allow it to penetrate deep into the beans but also liquid-like properties that dissolve 97–99% of the caffeine. The caffeine-laden CO<sub>2</sub> is then sprayed with high-pressure water to remove the caffeine. The caffeine can then be isolated by <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">charcoal</a> <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorption</a> (as above) or by <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recrystallization_(chemistry)" title="Recrystallization (chemistry)">recrystallization</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Reverse_osmosis" title="Reverse osmosis">reverse osmosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Decaffeination_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-Decaffeination-165">[165]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Extraction by organic solvents:</b> Certain organic solvents such as <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_acetate" title="Ethyl acetate">ethyl acetate</a> present much less health and environmental hazard than chlorinated and aromatic organic solvents used formerly. Another method is to use triglyceride oils obtained from spent coffee grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Decaffeination_165-2"><a href="#cite_note-Decaffeination-165">[165]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>"Decaffeinated" coffees do in fact contain caffeine in many cases – some commercially available decaffeinated coffee products contain considerable levels. One study found that decaffeinated coffee contained 10 mg of caffeine per cup, compared to approximately 85 mg of caffeine per cup for regular coffee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17132260_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17132260-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection_in_body_fluids">Detection in body fluids</span></h3>
<p>Caffeine can be quantified in blood, plasma, or serum to monitor therapy in neonates, confirm a diagnosis of poisoning, or facilitate a medicolegal death investigation. Plasma caffeine levels are usually in the range of 2–10 mg/L in coffee drinkers, 12–36 mg/L in neonates receiving treatment for apnea, and 40–400 mg/L in victims of acute overdosage. Urinary caffeine concentration is frequently measured in competitive sports programs, for which a level in excess of 15 mg/L is usually considered to represent abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analogs">Analogs</span></h3>
<p>Some analog substances have been created which mimic caffeine's properties with either function or structure or both. Of the latter group are the <a href="/wiki/Xanthine" title="Xanthine">xanthines</a> <a href="/wiki/DMPX" title="DMPX">DMPX</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DMPX_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-DMPX-168">[168]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8-chlorotheophylline" title="8-chlorotheophylline">8-chlorotheophylline</a>, which is an ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Dimenhydrinate" title="Dimenhydrinate">dramamine</a>. Members of a class of nitrogen substituted xanthines are often proposed as potential alternatives to caffeine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (November 2015)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> Many other xanthine analogues constituting the adenosine receptor antagonist class have also been elucidated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some other caffeine analogs:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dipropylcyclopentylxanthine" title="Dipropylcyclopentylxanthine">Dipropylcyclopentylxanthine</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/8-Cyclopentyl-1,3-dimethylxanthine" title="8-Cyclopentyl-1,3-dimethylxanthine">8-Cyclopentyl-1,3-dimethylxanthine</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/8-Phenyltheophylline" title="8-Phenyltheophylline">8-Phenyltheophylline</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precipitation_of_tannins">Precipitation of tannins</span></h3>
<p>Caffeine, as do other alkaloids such as <a href="/wiki/Cinchonine" title="Cinchonine">cinchonine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">quinine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Strychnine" title="Strychnine">strychnine</a>, precipitates polyphenols and <a href="/wiki/Tannin" title="Tannin">tannins</a>. This property can be used in a quantitation method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_occurrence">Natural occurrence</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coffee_beans2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5792" data-file-width="8688" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Coffee_beans2.jpg/220px-Coffee_beans2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Coffee_beans2.jpg/330px-Coffee_beans2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Coffee_beans2.jpg/440px-Coffee_beans2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coffee_beans2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roasted coffee beans</div></div></div>
<p>Around sixty plant species are known to contain caffeine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Common sources are the "beans" (seeds) of the two cultivated coffee plants, <i><a href="/wiki/Coffea_arabica" title="Coffea arabica">Coffea arabica</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Coffea_canephora" title="Coffea canephora">Coffea canephora</a></i> (the quantity varies, but 1.3% is a typical value<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>); in the leaves of the <a href="/wiki/Camellia_sinensis" title="Camellia sinensis">tea plant</a>; and in <a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">kola nuts</a>. Other sources include <a href="/wiki/Ilex_vomitoria" title="Ilex vomitoria">yaupon holly</a> leaves, South American holly <a href="/wiki/Yerba_mate" title="Yerba mate">yerba mate</a> leaves, seeds from Amazonian maple <a href="/wiki/Guarana" title="Guarana">guarana</a> berries, and Amazonian holly <a href="/wiki/Ilex_guayusa" title="Ilex guayusa">guayusa</a> leaves. Temperate climates around the world have produced unrelated caffeine-containing plants.
</p><p>Caffeine in plants acts as a natural <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a>: it can paralyze and kill predator insects feeding on the plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insecticide_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-insecticide-174">[174]</a></sup> High caffeine levels are found in coffee seedlings when they are developing foliage and lack mechanical protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> In addition, high caffeine levels are found in the surrounding soil of coffee seedlings, which inhibits seed germination of nearby coffee seedlings, thus giving seedlings with the highest caffeine levels fewer competitors for existing resources for survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>  Caffeine is stored in tea leaves in two places. Firstly, in the cell <a href="/wiki/Vacuole" title="Vacuole">vacuoles</a> where it is complexed with <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a>. This caffeine probably is released into the mouth parts of insects, to discourage herbivory. Secondly, around the vascular bundles, where it probably inhibits pathogenic fungi from entering and colonizing the vascular bundles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Caffeine in nectar may improve the reproductive success of the <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> producing plants by enhancing the reward memory of pollinators such as <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bees</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23471406_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23471406-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>The differing perceptions in the effects of ingesting beverages made from various plants containing caffeine could be explained by the fact that these beverages also contain varying mixtures of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylxanthine" title="Methylxanthine">methylxanthine</a> <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloids</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiac" title="Cardiac">cardiac</a> stimulants <a href="/wiki/Theophylline" title="Theophylline">theophylline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theobromine" title="Theobromine">theobromine</a>, and polyphenols that can form insoluble complexes with caffeine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref-1_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref-1-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Which is this—a book or a journal? If a book, which page? If a journal, a link can help verify this. (January 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<td><span class="nowrap">250 mL (8.5 US fl oz)</span>
</td>
<td><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001800000000000000♠</span>80
</td>
<td><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002320000000000000♠</span>320
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Products containing caffeine are coffee, tea, soft drinks ("colas"), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_drinks" title="Energy drinks">energy drinks</a>, other beverages, <a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">chocolate</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matissek_R_1997_175–84_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matissek_R_1997_175–84-186">[186]</a></sup> caffeine tablets, other oral products, and inhalation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beverages">Beverages</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coffee">Coffee</span></h4>
<p>The world's primary source of caffeine is the coffee "bean" (the seed of the <a href="/wiki/Coffea" title="Coffea">coffee plant</a>), from which coffee is brewed. Caffeine content in coffee varies widely depending on the type of <a href="/wiki/Coffee_bean" title="Coffee bean">coffee bean</a> and the method of preparation used;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICO_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICO-187">[187]</a></sup> even beans within a given bush can show variations in concentration. In general, one serving of coffee ranges from 80 to 100 milligrams, for a single shot (30 milliliters) of arabica-variety <a href="/wiki/Espresso" title="Espresso">espresso</a>, to approximately 100–125 milligrams for a cup (120 milliliters) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drip_coffee" title="Drip coffee">drip coffee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caffaq_roast_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-caffaq_roast-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jeremiahspick_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-jeremiahspick-189">[189]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Coffea_arabica" title="Coffea arabica">Arabica</a></i> coffee typically contains half the caffeine of the <i><a href="/wiki/Coffea_canephora" title="Coffea canephora">robusta</a></i> variety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICO_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-ICO-187">[187]</a></sup>
In general, dark-roast coffee has very slightly less caffeine than lighter roasts because the roasting process reduces caffeine content of the bean by a small amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caffaq_roast_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-caffaq_roast-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jeremiahspick_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-jeremiahspick-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tea">Tea</span></h4>
<p>Tea contains more caffeine than coffee by dry weight. A typical serving, however, contains much less, since less of the product is used as compared to an equivalent serving of coffee. Also contributing to caffeine content are growing conditions, processing techniques, and other variables. Thus, teas contain varying amounts of caffeine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRI_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FRI-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tea contains small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Theobromine" title="Theobromine">theobromine</a> and slightly higher levels of <a href="/wiki/Theophylline" title="Theophylline">theophylline</a> than coffee. Preparation and many other factors have a significant impact on tea, and color is a very poor indicator of caffeine content. Teas like the pale Japanese <a href="/wiki/Green_tea" title="Green tea">green tea</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gyokuro" title="Gyokuro">gyokuro</a></i>, for example, contain far more caffeine than much darker teas like <i><a href="/wiki/Lapsang_souchong" title="Lapsang souchong">lapsang souchong</a></i>, which has very little.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRI_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FRI-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soft_drinks_and_energy_drinks">Soft drinks and energy drinks</span></h4>
<p>Caffeine is also a common ingredient of <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Cola" title="Cola">cola</a>, originally prepared from <a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">kola nuts</a>. Soft drinks typically contain 0 to 55 milligrams of caffeine per 12 ounce serving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Energy_drink" title="Energy drink">energy drinks</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Red_Bull" title="Red Bull">Red Bull</a>, can start at 80 milligrams of caffeine per serving. The caffeine in these drinks either originates from the ingredients used or is an additive derived from the product of <a href="/wiki/Decaffeination" title="Decaffeination">decaffeination</a> or from chemical synthesis. Guarana, a prime ingredient of energy drinks, contains large amounts of caffeine with small amounts of theobromine and theophylline in a naturally occurring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slow-release" title="Slow-release">slow-release</a> <a href="/wiki/Excipient" title="Excipient">excipient</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_beverages">Other beverages</span></h4>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mate_(beverage)" title="Mate (beverage)">Mate</a> is a drink popular in many parts of South America. Its preparation consists of filling a gourd with the leaves of the South American holly <a href="/wiki/Yerba_mate" title="Yerba mate">yerba mate</a>, pouring hot but not boiling water over the leaves, and drinking with a straw, the bombilla, which acts as a filter so as to draw only the liquid and not the yerba leaves.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guaran%C3%A1" title="Guaraná">Guaraná</a> seeds ("beans") are used in making the commercially sold beverage <a href="/wiki/Guaran%C3%A1_Antarctica" title="Guaraná Antarctica">Guaraná Antarctica</a>, which originated in Brazil and is currently the fifteenth most popular soft drink in the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>The leaves of <i><a href="/wiki/Ilex_guayusa" title="Ilex guayusa">Ilex guayusa</a></i>, the Ecuadorian holly tree, are placed in boiling water to make a guayusa tea, which is both brewed locally and sold commercially throughout the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chocolate">Chocolate</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">Chocolate</a> derived from cocoa beans contains a small amount of caffeine. The weak stimulant effect of chocolate may be due to a combination of theobromine and theophylline, as well as caffeine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15549276_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15549276-193">[193]</a></sup> A typical 28-gram serving of a milk <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_bar" title="Chocolate bar">chocolate bar</a> has about as much caffeine as a cup of decaffeinated coffee. By weight, <a href="/wiki/Dark_chocolate" title="Dark chocolate">dark chocolate</a> has one to two times the amount of caffeine as coffee: 80–160 mg per 100 g. Higher percentages of cocoa such as 90% amount to 200 mg per 100 g approximately and thus, a 100-gram 85% cocoa chocolate bar contains about 195 mg caffeine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erowid_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erowid-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tablets">Tablets</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:No-Doz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1975" data-file-width="1475" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/No-Doz.jpg/220px-No-Doz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/No-Doz.jpg/330px-No-Doz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/No-Doz.jpg/440px-No-Doz.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:No-Doz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>No-Doz 100 mg caffeine tablets</div></div></div>
<p>Tablets offer several advantages over coffee, tea, and other caffeinated beverages, including convenience, known dosage, and avoidance of concomitant intake of sugar, acids, and fluids. Manufacturers of caffeine tablets claim that using caffeine of pharmaceutical quality improves mental alertness.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  These tablets are commonly used by students studying for their exams and by people who work or drive for long hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_oral_products">Other oral products</span></h3>
<p>One U.S. company is marketing oral dissolvable caffeine strips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> Another intake route is <a href="/wiki/SpazzStick" title="SpazzStick">SpazzStick</a>, a caffeinated <a href="/wiki/Lip_balm" title="Lip balm">lip balm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Alert Energy Caffeine Gum was introduced in the United States in 2013, but was voluntarily withdrawn after an announcement of an investigation by the FDA of the health effects of added caffeine in foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT5813_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT5813-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inhalants">Inhalants</span></h3>
<p>There are several products being marketed that offer inhalers that deliver proprietary blends of supplements, with caffeine being a key ingredient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> In 2012, the FDA sent a warning letter to one of the companies marketing these inhalers, expressing concerns for the lack of safety information available about inhaled caffeine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combinations_with_other_drugs">Combinations with other drugs</span></h3>
<ul><li>Some beverages combine <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> with caffeine to create a <a href="/wiki/Caffeinated_alcoholic_drink" title="Caffeinated alcoholic drink">caffeinated alcoholic drink</a>.  The stimulant effects of caffeine may mask the <a href="/wiki/Depressant" title="Depressant">depressant</a> effects of alcohol, potentially reducing the user's awareness of their level of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication" title="Alcohol intoxication">intoxication</a>.  Such beverages have been the subject of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ban_on_caffeinated_alcoholic_beverages" title="Ban on caffeinated alcoholic beverages">bans</a> due to safety concerns.  In particular, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="United States Food and Drug Administration">United States Food and Drug Administration</a> has classified caffeine added to malt liquor beverages as an "unsafe food additive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ya_ba" title="Ya ba">Ya ba</a> contains a combination of <a href="/wiki/Methamphetamine" title="Methamphetamine">methamphetamine</a> and caffeine.</li>
<li>Painkillers such as <a href="/wiki/Propyphenazone/paracetamol/caffeine" title="Propyphenazone/paracetamol/caffeine">propyphenazone/paracetamol/caffeine</a> combine caffeine with an <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">analgesic</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_spread_of_use">Discovery and spread of use</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kahvihuone.jpg"><img alt="An old photo of a dozen old and middle-aged men sitting on the ground around a mat. A man in front sits next to a mortar and holds a bat, ready for grinding. A man opposite to him holds a long spoon." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="597" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Kahvihuone.jpg/220px-Kahvihuone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Kahvihuone.jpg/330px-Kahvihuone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Kahvihuone.jpg/440px-Kahvihuone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kahvihuone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Coffeehouse" title="Coffeehouse">Coffeehouse</a> in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Palestine#Ottoman_era" title="History of Palestine">Palestine</a>, circa 1900</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_chocolate" title="History of chocolate">History of chocolate</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_coffee" title="History of coffee">History of coffee</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_tea" title="History of tea">History of tea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_yerba_mate" title="History of yerba mate">History of yerba mate</a></div>
<p>According to Chinese legend, the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Chinese emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Shennong</a>, reputed to have reigned in about 3000 BCE, inadvertently discovered tea when he noted that when certain leaves fell into boiling water, a fragrant and restorative drink resulted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Shennong is also mentioned in Lu Yu's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cha_Jing" title="Cha Jing">Cha Jing</a></i>, a famous early work on the subject of tea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest credible evidence of either coffee drinking or knowledge of the coffee plant appears in the middle of the fifteenth century, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> monasteries of the <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemenin</a> southern Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett-203">[203]</a></sup> From <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mokha" title="Mokha">Mocha</a>, coffee spread to <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Egypt</a> and North Africa, and by the 16th century, it had reached the rest of the Middle East, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_Empire" title="Safavid Empire">Persia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Turkey</a>.  From the Middle East, coffee drinking spread to Italy, then to the rest of Europe, and coffee plants were transported by the Dutch to the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a> and to the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyers_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyers-204">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">Kola nut</a> use appears to have ancient origins. It is chewed in many <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West African</a> cultures, in both private and social settings, to restore vitality and ease hunger pangs.
</p><p>The earliest evidence of <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa bean</a> use comes from residue found in an <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">ancient Mayan</a> pot dated to 600 BCE. Also, <a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">chocolate</a> was consumed in a bitter and spicy drink called <i>xocolatl</i>, often seasoned with <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">vanilla</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chile_pepper" title="Chile pepper">chile pepper</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achiote" title="Achiote">achiote</a>. <i>Xocolatl</i> was believed to fight fatigue, a belief probably attributable to the theobromine and caffeine content. Chocolate was an important luxury good throughout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, and cocoa beans were often used as currency.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><i>Xocolatl</i> was introduced to <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_people" title="Spanish people">Spaniards</a>, and became a popular beverage by 1700. The Spaniards also introduced the <a href="/wiki/Theobroma_cacao" title="Theobroma cacao">cacao tree</a> into the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>. It was used in <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemical</a> processes, where it was known as "black bean".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The leaves and stems of the yaupon holly (<i><a href="/wiki/Ilex_vomitoria" title="Ilex vomitoria">Ilex vomitoria</a></i>) were used by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a> to brew a <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a> called <i>asi</i> or the "<a href="/wiki/Black_drink" title="Black drink">black drink</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fairbanks_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fairbanks-205">[205]</a></sup> Archaeologists have found evidence of this use far into antiquity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> possibly dating to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_period_in_the_Americas" title="Archaic period in the Americas">Late Archaic times</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fairbanks_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fairbanks-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Chemical_identification.2C_isolation.2C_and_synthesis"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_identification,_isolation,_and_synthesis">Chemical identification, isolation, and synthesis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1986" data-file-width="1638" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier.jpg/170px-Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier.jpg/255px-Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier.jpg/340px-Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pierre Joseph Pelletier</div></div></div>
<p>In 1819, the German chemist <a href="/wiki/Friedlieb_Ferdinand_Runge" title="Friedlieb Ferdinand Runge">Friedlieb Ferdinand Runge</a> isolated relatively pure caffeine for the first time; he called it <i>"Kaffebase"</i> (i.e., a <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> that exists in coffee).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> According to Runge, he did this at the behest of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weinberg_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-weinberg-209">[209]</a></sup> In 1821, caffeine was isolated both by the French chemist <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Jean_Robiquet" title="Pierre Jean Robiquet">Pierre Jean Robiquet</a> and by another pair of French chemists, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier" title="Pierre Joseph Pelletier">Pierre-Joseph Pelletier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bienaim%C3%A9_Caventou" title="Joseph Bienaimé Caventou">Joseph Bienaimé Caventou</a>, according to Swedish chemist <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a> in his yearly journal. Furthermore, Berzelius stated that the French chemists had made their discoveries independently of any knowledge of Runge's or each other's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> However, Berzelius later acknowledged Runge's priority in the extraction of caffeine, stating:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> "However, at this point, it should not remain unmentioned that Runge (in his <i>Phytochemical Discoveries</i>, 1820, pages 146–147) specified the same method and described caffeine under the name <i>Caffeebase</i> a year earlier than Robiquet, to whom the discovery of this substance is usually attributed, having made the first oral announcement about it at a meeting of the Pharmacy Society in Paris."
</p><p>Pelletier's article on caffeine was the first to use the term in print (in the French form <i>Caféine</i> from the French word for coffee: <i>café</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> It corroborates Berzelius's account:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Caffeine, noun (feminine). Crystallizable substance discovered in coffee in 1821 by Mr. Robiquet. During the same period – while they were searching for quinine in coffee because coffee is considered by several doctors to be a medicine that reduces fevers and because coffee belongs to the same family as the cinchona [quinine] tree – on their part, Messrs. Pelletier and Caventou obtained caffeine; but because their research had a different goal and because their research had not been finished, they left priority on this subject to Mr. Robiquet. We do not know why Mr. Robiquet has not published the analysis of coffee which he read to the Pharmacy Society. Its publication would have allowed us to make caffeine better known and give us accurate ideas of coffee's composition ...
</p></blockquote>
<p>Robiquet was one of the first to isolate and describe the properties of pure caffeine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> whereas Pelletier was the first to perform an <a href="/wiki/Elemental_analysis" title="Elemental analysis">elemental analysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1827, M. Oudry isolated "théine" from tea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> but it was later proved by <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Johannes_Mulder" title="Gerardus Johannes Mulder">Mulder</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> and by Carl Jobst<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> that theine was actually the same as caffeine.
</p><p>In 1895, German chemist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermann_Emil_Fischer" title="Hermann Emil Fischer">Hermann Emil Fischer</a> (1852–1919) first synthesized caffeine from its chemical components (i.e. a "<a href="/wiki/Total_synthesis" title="Total synthesis">total synthesis</a>"), and two years later, he also derived the structural formula of the compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> This was part of the work for which Fischer was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize</a> in 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_regulations">Historic regulations</span></h3>
<p>Because it was recognized that coffee contained some compound that acted as a stimulant, first coffee and later also caffeine has sometimes been subject to regulation. For example, in the 16th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamist" title="Islamist">Islamists</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> made coffee illegal for some classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agostonmasters2009_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-agostonmasters2009-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hopkins2006_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-hopkins2006-222">[222]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II of England</a> tried to ban it in 1676,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pendergrast13_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pendergrast13-224">[224]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_II_of_Prussia" title="Frederick II of Prussia">Frederick II of Prussia</a> banned it in 1777,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pendergrast11_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pendergrast11-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> and coffee was banned in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> at various times between 1756 and 1823.
</p><p>In 1911, caffeine became the focus of one of the earliest documented health scares, when the US government seized 40 barrels and 20 kegs of <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> syrup in <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga,_Tennessee" title="Chattanooga, Tennessee">Chattanooga, Tennessee</a>, alleging the caffeine in its drink was "injurious to health".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2010614_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid2010614-227">[227]</a></sup> Although the judge ruled in favor of Coca-Cola, two bills were introduced to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a> in 1912 to amend the <a href="/wiki/Pure_Food_and_Drug_Act" title="Pure Food and Drug Act">Pure Food and Drug Act</a>, adding caffeine to the list of "habit-forming" and "deleterious" substances, which must be listed on a product's label.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulations">Regulations</span></h3>
<p>The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) in the United States currently allows only beverages containing less than 0.02% caffeine;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> but caffeine powder, which is sold as a dietary supplement, is unregulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> It is a regulatory requirement that the label of most prepackaged foods must declare a list of ingredients, including food additives such as caffeine, in descending order of proportion. However, there is no regulatory provision for mandatory quantitative labeling of caffeine, (e.g., milligrams caffeine per stated serving size). There are a number of food ingredients that naturally contain caffeine. These ingredients must appear in food ingredient lists. However, as is the case for "food additive caffeine", there is no requirement to identify the quantitative amount of caffeine in composite foods containing ingredients that are natural sources of caffeine. While coffee or chocolate are broadly recognized as caffeine sources, some ingredients (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Guarana" title="Guarana">guarana</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yerba_mat%C3%A9" title="Yerba maté">yerba maté</a>) are likely less recognized as caffeine sources. For these natural sources of caffeine, there is no regulatory provision requiring that a food label identify the presence of caffeine nor state the amount of caffeine present in the food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span></h3>
<p>Global consumption of caffeine has been estimated at 120,000 tonnes per year, making it the world's most popular psychoactive substance. This amounts to one serving of a caffeinated beverage for every person every day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc.net_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc.net-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religions">Religions</span></h3>
<p>Some  <a href="/wiki/Church_of_God_(Restoration)" title="Church of God (Restoration)">Church of God (Restoration)</a> adherents, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Scientists" title="Christian Scientists">Christian Scientists</a> do not consume caffeine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Until recently, the <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">Seventh-day Adventist Church</a> asked for its members to "abstain from caffeinated drinks", but has removed this from <a href="/wiki/Baptismal_vows" title="Baptismal vows">baptismal vows</a> (while still recommending abstention as policy).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Some from these religions believe that one is not supposed to consume a non-medical, psychoactive substance, or believe that one is not supposed to consume a substance that is addictive. <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> has said the following with regard to caffeinated beverages: " . . . the Church revelation spelling out health practices (Doctrine and Covenants 89) does not mention the use of caffeine.  The Church's health guidelines prohibit alcoholic drinks, smoking or chewing of tobacco, and 'hot drinks' – taught by Church leaders to refer specifically to tea and coffee."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gaudiya_Vaishnavism" title="Gaudiya Vaishnavism">Gaudiya Vaishnavas</a> generally also abstain from caffeine, because they believe it clouds the mind and over-stimulates the senses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> To be initiated under a guru, one must have had no caffeine, alcohol, nicotine or other drugs, for at least a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeinated beverages are widely consumed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> today. In the 16th century, some Muslim authorities made unsuccessful attempts to ban them as forbidden "intoxicating beverages" under <a href="/wiki/Islamic_dietary_laws" title="Islamic dietary laws">Islamic dietary laws</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campo2009_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campo2009-237">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2011_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2011-238">[238]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_organisms">Other organisms</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caffeinated_spiderwebs_modified.jpg"><img alt="Caffeine effects on spider webs" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="555" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Caffeinated_spiderwebs_modified.jpg/200px-Caffeinated_spiderwebs_modified.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Caffeinated_spiderwebs_modified.jpg/300px-Caffeinated_spiderwebs_modified.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Caffeinated_spiderwebs_modified.jpg/400px-Caffeinated_spiderwebs_modified.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caffeinated_spiderwebs_modified.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caffeine effects on <a href="/wiki/Spider_web" title="Spider web">spider webs</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_psychoactive_drugs_on_animals" title="Effect of psychoactive drugs on animals">Effect of psychoactive drugs on animals</a></div>
<p>Recently discovered bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_putida" title="Pseudomonas putida">Pseudomonas putida</a></i> CBB5 can live on pure caffeine and can cleave caffeine into carbon dioxide and ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caffeine is toxic to birds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> and to dogs and cats,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> and has a pronounced adverse effect on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a>, various insects, and <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> This is at least partly due to a poor ability to metabolize the compound, causing higher levels for a given dose per unit weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20859793_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20859793-140">[140]</a></sup> Caffeine has also been found to enhance the reward memory of <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bees</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23471406_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23471406-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<p>Caffeine has been used to double chromosomes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18464846_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18464846-243">[243]</a></sup>
</p>

This decision is disputed by many and is not supported by any convincing body of experimental evidence. ...
